J.  Diabursoments
3.48     Disbursements for vehicles, processing equipment and machinery,
breeding stock, construction mdaterials, laboratory equipment, fishmeal,
eel feed and fertilizer would be at 100% of the foreign exchange costs of
imported items or 100% of the ex-factory price of locally manufactured
items and 75% of local expenditures for other items procured locally.
Disbursements for overseas training and study tours and consultant
services would be 100% of total costs. For the Jiangsu component,
disbursements for pond development, laver establishment, plantation
establishment and on-farm works would be at 37% of total expenditures;
disbursements for roads and buildings would be at 24% of total
expenditures. For the Zhejiang component, disbursements for sea dikes
would be at 65% of total expenditures; pond development at 36% and roads
and buildings at 34%. For expenditures relating to civil works, overseas
training, study tours, and contracts for goods and services costing less
than $200,000 equivalent, reimbursement will be made on the basis of
statements of expenditure (SOEs). SOEs would be supported by progress
reports showing physical quantities and unit prices which would be updated
annually by the Project Offices and reviewed by IDA. Supporting
documentation would be retained by the Project Offices and be made
available to Bank Group supervision missions.
3.49     In order to provide for efficient disbursement, special accounts
would be opened in US dollars in banks acceptable to IDA with initial
deposits of SDR 3.6 million (US$4.9 million) and SDR 1.9 million (US$2.6
million) which represents the average estimated four-month expenditures
for Jiangsu and Zhejiang, respectively. Applications for replenishment of
the special accounts would be submitted quarterly, or whenever the special
accounts are drawn down to 50% of their initial deposit, whichever comes
first.
3.50     The project is expected to be completed by end 1992 and the
closing date would be June 30, 1993. The disbursement experience in China
has been very favorable and better than Bank-wide and regional averages.
The project disbursement profile is in line with that for specific
investment loans in China. In order to facilitate startup of the project,
retroactive financing is being provided. This would not exceed SDR 5.1
million (US$6.9 million) for Jiangsu and SDR 2.2 (US$3.1 million) for
Zhejiang and would cover expenditures made between appraisal (June 30,
1987) and signing of the Loan/Credit. Estimated schedules of expenditures
and disbursements are given in Annex 3.
K. Accounts and Audits
3.51     In Jiangsu, records of expenditures on civil works, machinery and
vehicles, construction and electrification materials, and livestock would
be maintained by the project offices in participating counties. These
records would be forwarded to the municipal development corporations,
which would prepare consolidated accounts including their own records of
expenditures on civil works and agroprocessing equipment. The provincial
project office would, in turn, compile a complete report on project
expenditures. In Zhejiang, records of local expenditures would be
maintained by the three participating development corporations (Zhejiang



- 26 -
Land Reclamation Corp., Zhejiang Aquaculture Corp., and the Ningbo-Damutu
Coastal Land Development Corporation) and forwarded to the project offices
in the provincial Water Resources Bureau, Bureau of Aquatic Prodtcts, and
the Ningbo municipal project office, respectively. The municipal project
offices (Jiangsu) and provincial bureau project offices (Zhejiang) would
include their own records of expenditures and forward all material to the
respective provincial project office for compilation of a complete
report. The provincial POs would submit reports to the Bank Group.
Assurances were obtained at negotiations that the provincial POs would
maintain the consolidated accounts for auditing annually by the State
Audit Agency and that the audited accounts would be submitted to the Bank
Group within six months of the close of each financial year. The audited
accounts would include details of withdrawals from the Loan/Credit account
made on the basis of SOEs and the auditors' opinion as to whether such
withdrawals were against expenditures eligible for reimbursement by the
Bank Group.
L. Environmental lmnact
3.52     In Jiangsu province, large tracts along the coastline have been
converted from beaches and mudflats into agricultural land. The project
areas are all on reclaimed land; hence the project will not infringe on
any natural wetlands. There is a large wintering area for migrating birds
adjoining the proposed project area, however, development activities under
the project would not adversely affect wildlife. The Zhejiang coastal
area, lacking marshes, ponds and extensive mudflats, is unsuitable for
wildfowl. Provision for waste control is being included in the design of
the agroprocessing plans. The inclusion of mink raising in the project
is an important part of waste control; their willingness to eat normally
inedible material makes their producers a significant market for waste
products such as reject eggs, fisheries scraps and packinghouse wastes.
An assurance was obtained at negotiations that the provincial governments
would insure that project activities would conform to appropriate
guidelines on environmental control issued by the provincial governments
and accept.-ble to the Bank Group.



- 27 -
IV. PROJECT ORGANIZATION AND NANAGEMENT
A. Prolect OrganizatioM
4.1         In line with the trend toward decentralization of government
activities, the governments of Jiangsu and Zhejiang provinces would be
responsible for overall project implementation. Each province has
established a Provincial Coordinating Committee (PCC) for inter-agency
coordination, a Provincial-level Project Office (PPO) to function as a
secretariat to the PCC and as an administrative organ for obtaining
government approvals, and Development Corporations (DCs), provincial
autonomous entities to serve as implementing agencies for the project.
The Bank Group's legal documents (Loan, Credit and Project Agreements)
distinguish between the two provinces' components by specifying in the
project description Part A those activities carried out in Jiangsu
province and Part B, those in Zhejiang province. The Schedules for
Allocation of the Loan and Credit have specified categories and amounts
for Parts A and B, separately, and there would be two special accounts.
The Government and each province would enter into Subsidiary Loan
Agreements setting out their respective obligations in project
implementation. Signing of the Subsidiary Loan Agreements with each of
the provinces, on terms and conditions satisfactory to IDA, would be a
condition of Loan and Credit effectiveness. The organizat-ion for project
implementation is shown in Charts 1 and 2 for the respective provinces.
B. Provincial Coordinating Committees and Project Offices
4.2         Each province has set up a PCC chaired by a vice governor and
consisting of representatives from provincial level technical and
financial agencies involved in the project. The PCCs would act as policy,
planning and review boards and serve to enlist the support of the agencies
represented. The PCCs would meet periodically as needed to coordinate the
activities of the agencies involved in project implementation and to make
project-related policy decisions. They would approve the annual work
program and budget and would also serve to keep the provincial governments
apprised of overall progress in project implementation.
4.3         PPOs have been established within the main agencies involved
in planning and preparing the project--the Bureau of Aquatic Products in
Jiangsu and the Planning Commission in Zhejiang. PPOs would be
responsible for submitting annual implementation plans and budgets for the
PCC approval, monitoring physical and financial progress of the project
(including setting up the accounting system, training accountants and
collecting documentation from participating entities for disbursement),
and establishing guidelines for quality control and labor safety. They
would be the coordinators of the various agencies involved in the project
for procurement, training, and supply of government allocated materials.
The PPOs would approve disbursement of Bank Group and provincial
government funds by the provincial Finance Bureaus, and monitor project
execution and repayment of funds by executing agencies. The PPOs would
carry out the compilation of data for Progress Reports submitted to the
PCCs and to the Bank Group and would be the liaison points with the Bank
Group.



- 28 -
4.4         The PPOs have drawn experienced staff from the Bureaus of
Water Resources, Aquatic Products, and Agriculture and from the Planning
Commissions. While the directorships would be part-time positions filled
by senior provincial government officials, deputy directors would be
government officials assigned full-time to the PPO and responsible for the
day-to-day work. Each PPO would have about 10 staff, including civil
engineers, aquaculturists, accountants, statisticians and an interpreter.
4.5         In Jiangsu, a structure of coordinating committees and project
offices similar to that at the provincial level has been established at
the municipal level in Lianyungang, Nantong and Yancheng and in the
participating counties under their jurisdiction. The Municipal-level
Project Offices (MPOs) would monitor physical and financial progress of
the project, provide guidelines on construction standards, and approve
disbursement of funds by the municipal Finance Bureaus to the development
companies within their districts and to the county Finance Bureaus. The
County Project Offices (CPOs) in the 18 participating counties would be
responsible for site selection, development planning, financial analysis,
and coordination of the participating townships. They would prepare
preliminary site surveys and designs, approve construction contracts, and
annual budgets. They would also, with the assistance of the MPO,
supervise construction progress and coordinate loan collection from county
and township farms and enterprises (paras. 4.11-4.12). CPOs would also
approve disbursements to the county-level development companies and the
townships. Both MPOs and CPOs would be staffed with 7-10 qualified
livestock specialists, aquaculturists, agronomists, civil engineers,
economists and accountants.
4.6         In Zhejiang, where the scope of the project is smaller and
organizationally less complex, project offices have been set up in the two
government agencies--the Bureaus of Water Resources and Aquatic
Products--and in Ningbo municipality. The staffing and responsibilities
would be similar to the Jiangsu NPOs. CPOs in the Bureau of Water
Resources and the Bureau of Aquatic Products would be similar to the
Jiangsu CPOs.
C. DLevelopaent Corporations
4.7         DCs are a new form of organization to China and have been
created as a means of separating government's normal administrative and
regulatory functions from developmental and commercial activities. In line
with central government directives, both provinces have designated DCs for
the dual roles of project implementing agencies and financial
intermediaries. In Jiangsu, there are three municipal-level DCs: the
Lianyungang Aquatic Breeding Corporation, the Nantong General Aluatic
Products Corporation and the Yancheng General Development Corporation.
While the project has been the impetus for establishment of the Yancheng
corporation, both Lianyungang and Nantong are utilizing previously
established corporations for the project. In Zhejiang, the Zhejiang Tidal
Land Reclamation Corporation, the Zhejiang Aquaculture Corporation and the
Ningbo-Damutu Coastal Land Development Corporation would serve as agents
of the provincial government in implementing the project. All six



- 29 -
corporations have charters consistent with regulations approved by the
State Council, giving them the status of legal entities. The charters
detail the scope of activities, sources and amount of capital, and
borrowing powers. They describe management structure, including
provision for boards of directors and professional managers. The six
charters have been reviewed and are acceptable to the Bank Group. It
was confirmed at negotiations that all six DCs under the project have
secured the necessary approvals, licenses and initial funding from the
appropriate provincial authorities and have been established.
4.8         The DCs have a mandate for a broad range oi. developmental
activities including implementation of the proposed and similar
projects; introduction of foreign capital and technology through joint
ventures and other forms of collaboration; marketing of inputs and
products; and provision of technical assistance. The provincial- and
muncipal-level DCs would implement the project and other activities
through subsidiary companies. These would irnclude county- and
township-level development companies, established with equity provided
by local governments and supervised by the provincial-/municipr;l-level
DCs, and wholly- or jointly-owned subsidiaries, such as new agro-
processing facilities, which would be organized and managed as
independent financial entities. The six DCs would rely on revenue
earning activities, loan financing and service fees for recovery of
development costs including servicing of foreign exchange borrowings;
since the DCs would bear the financial risk of subloans extended for
project activities, they would provide a reserve for bad debts. An
understanding was reached at negotiations on operating guidelines (Annex
8) for DC financial viability, including requirements that the DCs
maintain a debt-equity ratio of not greater than 5:1, that any
additional activities selected for DC investment participation and to be
financed under the project should have financia;L and economic rates of
return of at least 12%, and that sublending by DCs to enterprises and
farms would be at prevailing Agricultural Bank of China (ABC) interest
rates. DCs would pay profit and other taxes to the provincial and
municipal governments and allocate after-tax profits as determined by
the boards of directors.
D. Management of Production
4.9         Whereas the Project Offices would be active in planning and
implementing the project, their responsibilities after the construction
period would be mainly monitoring repayment from the DCs to the
financial bureaus and most of the staff would return to their original
agencies. The DCs would be responsible for overseeing production
activities (fishery, livestock and agricultural products) throughout the
life of the project. The DCs at the relevant level would contract with
county, township and village farms (collectives) for shrimp, eel and
laver production and would insure the availability of necessary
production inputs, including fry, raw materials for feed, and credit for
working capital. Shrimp farms would in turn contract with families or
groups of households under the production responsibility system for
operation of a single pond within the farm, set targets for production,
and recover their investment costs through contract fees. Individual



- 30 -
households would be responsible for meeting production targets and would
receive bonuses for above-target production. Eel farms and laver beds
would generally be organized as township or village enterprises,
operated through contracts (with the local government), which specify
land use rights, tenure and annual production targets. Profits from
above-target production would be shared by the management and workers.
The DCs would contract directly with individual households for livestock
production and with townships and villages for fruit and nut
production. Breeding farms would be managed as township or village
enterprises with permanent employees receiving salaries and bonuses. In
keeping with the ongoing economic reform process in China, in each case
incentives, responsibilities and production risks would be located to
the extent possible with the natural unit of production.
4.10        Agro-processing facilities, including feed mills and cold
stores, would be owned by the DCs, either wholly or as joint-ventures
with local governments, and operated as commercial activities with their
output priced at competitive market levels. Legally, they would have
the status of state-owned enterprises with charters registered with the
General Administration of Industry and Commerce (GAIC) defining the
scope of their activities, sources and amounts of capital, borrowing
powers and management structure. An assurance was obtained at
negotiations that the agro-processing facilities would be managed by
competent staff with experience and backgrounds relevant to the
respective types of processing.
E. inancial Management
Terms and Conditions
4.11        Proceeds of the IBRD Loan and IDA Credit would be onlent by
the Ministry of Finance (MOF) to the provincial governments of Jiangsu
and Zhejiang at a 4.5% annual interest rate, with repayment over 15
years including a five-year grace period. The provincial governments
would bear the foreign exchange risk. The Jiangsu provincial government
would onlend to the municipal governments of Lianyungang, Nantong and
Yancheng for the activities under their respective administrations. The
municipal governments would use a portion of the funds directly to cover
the costs of establishing technical centers (Lianyungang and Nantong)
and the remaining funds would be onlent to the municipal or county level
DCs in the form of subloans issued by the municipal governments through
their municipal or county finance bureaus. In Zhejiang, the provincial
government would onlend directly to the Zhejiang Aquatic Products
Corporation and the Land Reclamation Corporation for the activities
under their purview and to Ningbo municipal government, for further
onlending to the Ningbo-Damutu Coastal Land Development Corporation.
Lending to county-level DCs would be carried out with the respective
county as guarantor for repayment. End users--collectives (in the case
of shrimp, eel and laver) and county and township enterprises (for feed
mills, cold stores, and other agro-processing, and animal husbandry and
agricultural activities) would pay interest at prevailing ABC rates,
which currently range between 7.2% and 10.8%.



- 31 -
These rates are positive and are expected to remain positive.
Subbortou.ars would be required to contribute about 30% of total project
costs. During negotiations assurances were obtained that project funds
would be onlunt to subborrowers at the same interest rates charged by
ABC on subloans for similar purposes and with similar maturities and
that the interest rate would be subject to periodic review with the Bank
Group. An understanding was reached that the development corporations
would retain sufficient spreads to cover their transaction costs,
including a reasonable provision for bad debts. The flow r V.unds is
shown in Chart 3.
Cost Recover
4.12        Farms and enterprises would repay loans from DCs on
schedules determined by analysis of the investment cash flows. Local
branches of ABC would handle on behalf of the DCs both disbursement and
recovery of funds for a service charge to be agreed between the
parties. DCs would repay the municipalities and provinces, who would in
turn repay MOF for the use of the IBRD Loan and IDA Credit according to
the prescribed schedules. During negotiations, an assurance was
obtained that the "float", i.e. any net cash accruals by the DCs due to
differences in timing between subloan receipts and repayments to
Government, would be used by the DCs only for financing development
activities similar to those undertaken under the project. Under present
regulations for state enterprises, the DCs would pay taxes at normai
enterprise rates, but would otherwise be allowed to retain net profits.
Cost recovery for extension and training would be effected through
service fees.
P. Execution of Prolect Works
4.13        Within the framework of review artd approvals by the
provincial coordinating committees and project offices and the municipal
and provincial DCs and POs, county or township DCs would be responsible
for executing the project's pond development, land development, crop
establisbment, and animal breeding and production components, including
support facilities. Infrastructure, including water and power supplies,
roads, bridges and culverts, would be carried out by line agencias of
the lccal governments. In Zhejiang, sea dike construction would be
contracted under local competitive bidding procedures. Construction of
ponds would be contracted to construction enterprises and teams of local
farmers, depending on the component and local circumstances. MPOs under
the slpervision of the PPO would directly implement the higher-level
traitting program. The DCs would contract out the construction of
agro-processing facilities.
G. Project Launch Workshop
4.14        To initiate the implementation phase of the proposed
project, Bank and local staff would conduct a project launch workshop in
one of the project areas (to be selected) for staff of the PPOs, MPOs
and DCs who are responsible for carrying out the proposed project. The
purpose of the workshop would be to review the tota?. process of



- 32 -
implementation and to clarify individual tasks and responsibilities at
each level. Topics to be covered wou'd include: procurement and
contracting procedures, project financi.al management, and project
monitoring and evaluation.
H. Monitoring. Evaluation and Reporting
4.15        The provincial, municipal (Jiangsu) and agency POs
(Zhejiang) would monitor project progress and evaluate the project's
economic benefits and overall impact. In Jiangsu, each township or
village farm and enterprise would provide to the CPO information on the
following: progress in pond (or crop or livestock) development, yields
and production levels, marketing channels and prices, training, contract
terms and conditions, and incomes of project beneficiaries as well as
the extent of their participation in project activities. This
information would be compiled by each county and submitted to the MPO.
The NPO would use this information in preparing a quarterly report of
physical and financial progress, which would also include an account of
its own expenditures. This report would be submitted to the municipal
coordinating committee and to the PP0. The PP0 would review and
evaluate reports from each MPO and on this basis prepare a semi-annual
progress report for submission to the PCC and to the Bank Group.
Reporting on financial progress would include information on actual and
cumulative expenditures using a format similar to the detailed cost
estimates (Annex 3). Monitoring and evaluation by the POs and
coordinating committees at each level would be based on the quarterly
and semi-annual reports. Monitoring and evaluation methodology would be
one of the main topics of discussion at the project launch workshop to
be held at the outset of project implementation (para. 4.14).
4.16        Not later than six months following completion of
Lean/Credit disbursements, the PPOs would prepare for submission to the
Bank Group a Project Completion Report (PCR) in which project results
would be compared with appraisal estimates. The PCR would describe
changes in production technology, changes in prices in real terms,
changes in earnings and incomes of project farmers, changes in permanent
employment resulting from the project, and related cost recovery
issues. The economic and financial rates of return would be
recalculated on the basis of actual costs and re-estimated benefits.

V. PRODUCTION. MARKETING AND PRIGES
A. Production and Processinu
5.1      Yields and production in the project areas are summarized in
Table 5.1. Further details of production are given in Annex 6.
Aguaculture
5.2      ShiLp:   Shrimp farms, comprised of 20 to 870 ha aggregations of
2-3.3 ha ponds, would belong to villages, townships or counties. The
farms would be managed by a permanent staff of technicians, accountants,
and other support staff and would contract with households for pond
operation--generally about 1.6 ha to a worker. The techniques of shrimp
production would be similar in both provinces. Growing conditions would
be slightly more favorable in Zhejiang; growth cessation during the
hottest summer months would be less of a problem since water supplies
would be directly from the sea and thus water temperature would be lower,
and the growing season in Zhejiang is about two weeks longer due to its
more southerly location.
5.3      The project would address the main constraints to yield increases
and improved quality of traded produet: pond structure and water control,
nutritionally balanced feed, and proper handling from farm to market.
With the deeper ponds and better water control, the introduction of
controlled feeding practices and pelletized feed, average yields are
expected to increase from about 0.9 t/ha to 1.95 t/ha by the third year of
production. The yield structure in Jiangsu is projected to be about 308
in the 13-16 cm size range, 50% in the 10-13 cm range and 20% in the 8-10
cm range; in Zhejiang, it is projected to be 48% in the 13-16 cm size
range and 52% in the 10-13 cm range--slightly larger on account of the
longer growing season. Total incremental production at full development
would be around 12,200 t in Jiangsu and 2,700 t in Zhejiang. About 70% of
the shrimp are expected to meet the size requirements for export (10.5 cm
in Jiangsu and 12 cm in Zhejiang) and would be processed in headless,
shell-on form (about 6,300 t after removal. of the head). The remainder
(4,500 t with head) would be sold unprocessed in the local market.
5.4      Laver, a leafy edible seaweed better known by its Japanese name,
nori, would be harvested every two weeks from December until late spring.
Early harvests yield about 1,050 kg/ha, increasing up to 1,950 kg/ha
toward the end of the season, with an average of 1,500 kg/ha for each of
ten harvests. Annual yield is about 15 t/ha in wet weight or 1.5 t/ha in
dried form after processing. Total annual production is expected to be
about 410 t. Processing machines for automated washing and drying of
laver would be provided for each 10-15 ha, generally within 1/2 hr
distance from the culture sites.
5.5       W .  The species used for eel culture in China is the Japanese
eel, AnguIlla jagonica, a catadromous species (grows in freshwater but
migrates to the sea for spawning). Technically, it is still impossible to

produce elver in hatcheries and all eel culture makes use of seeds of wild
origin. Glass eel of & jiDonica occur only along the coast of China,
Korea, Japan and Taiwan. At present, Japan and Taiwan depend heavily on
glass eel and elver imported from Korea and China. Eel culture is
characterized by a combination of enclosed and outdoor ponds in a series
of different sizes, frequent grading, thinning and transfer from one pond
to another, and multiple size-selective harvests without any definite
seasonality. With aeration and continuous water replacement, very high
stocking density can be maintained. Japanese eels have highly variable
growth rates--fast growers reach market size (150-200 g) in less than 12
months from glass eel stage, while slow growers may take more than 24
months. Annual yields in Jiangsu are expected to reach 10 kg/m2 in
enclosed ponds, 15 t/ha in outdoor ponds, and 45 t/ha in power plant waste
water systems.
5.6       Zhejiang would have in addition to the standard combination of
outdoor and enclosed ponds, some 40 ha of ponds using slow-flowing power
plant waste water. This system would use enclosed ponds, roof covered
ponds, and open ponds. By using heated waste water from the power plants,
supplemented with steam from boilers, and cold surface water from
reservoirs during the summer, water temperature will be maintained year
round at 25 - 28 degrees C. Yield is projected to be 52.5 t/ha in the
heated flowthrough system in Zhejiang. Total annual production would be
1,660 t in Jiangsu and 3,150 t in Zhejiang. A export holding facility
would allow the eel to be conditioned for transport and packaged.
Animal Husbandry
5.7      Mink:  This component takes advantage of the availability of
animal waste (trash fish, goat and rabbit offal). The existing mink
production industry is of fairly high standard. Viability of mink
production is dependent on suitable strains of animals, reproductive
performance, feeding, and disease resistance. Size of the animals--and
thus ef the skin--is an important commercial characteristic. The
condition for size is good development from birth to grading time, and a
good size at weaning is thus the principal criterion of good skin size.
Successful mink farming is dependent on breeding animals with good
hereditary characteristics, the availability of good quality feed,
adequate professional skill of the mink breeder and necessary equipment
for raising. The project would address all these issues. At full
development, annual incremental production would be about 130,000 pelts.
Until recently, a major shortcoming to production efficiency has been the
lack of grading facilities--pelts were sold to the Foreign Trade
Commission without regard to quality. However, grading procedures have
now been set up in the area to provide further incentives to producers to
improve quality of production.
5.8      Goats:  The project would develop goat raising as the main
enterprise for 12,000 specialized households in 50 townships. Each
household would be .1located an average of 1.3 ha of grazing land to be
used in conjunction with about 0.5 ha of arable land, on which to produce
grain and fodder crops. At full development, households would sell an
average of 100 goats per year (eight months of age, 20 kg liveweight) to
the Township Diversified Economy Service Corporations for resale to the



- 36 -
centralized slaughterhouse being provided under the project. Total
incremental production under the project is estimated to be 1.0 million
goats, yielding about 800,000 skins suitable for processing by an existing
pigskin factory which would be expanded for this purpose and 10,000 t of
goat meat from the slaughterhouse to be provided under the project.
5.9      Ducks/fish:  The project would expand the existing 170 ha duck
production area with an additional 100 ha of ponds. Each pond would be
about 0.6 ha, managed by a single household and would produce four cycles
of 300 ducks annually. With a combination of duck manure, grass gathered
from an adjacent 300 ha tract, and compound feed, fish yield is expected
to be 5.25 t/ha with incremental production about 1,230 t. Total
incremental production would be about 460,000 ducks, yielding about 800 t
of meat and 5 t of down.
5.10     Rabbits:  Rabbit raising has been included in the project because
it would give quick returns, does not require specialized labor, and would
take advantage of the abundant grasses in the coastal regions. Some 8,240
households in 52 townships would each raise 130 rabbits a year. Two
breeding farms to be established under the project would provide parent
stock to farmers. Each household would start with 10 breeding females and
five breeding males to produce annually 130 meat rabbits to be raised for
five months and sold for commercial processing at existing facilities,
which are adequate to handle the increased output. Within three years,
one million rabbits would be produced annually.
Fruit and nut oroduction
5.11     Fruits and nuts were selected for expanded production under the
project, mainly to meet unfulfilled domestic demand. The project sites
are under the administration of Lianyungang and Yancheng municipalities,
with hawthorn to be planted in the hills of western Ganyu, the grape and
chestnut plantations over the sandy plains in southern Donghai, the
orchards in Binhai in the old Yellow River channel, ginkgo in Pixian, and
chestnuts in Donghai. Full development yields would be reached by the
third year for grapes, the fifth year for chestnuts, the reventh through
ninth year for orchards, and the fifteenth year for hawthorn and ginkgo.
The area expansion and yield increases, due to improved planting material,
better management and storage facilities provided by the project, are
expected to result in incremental production of 61,200 t of fruits and
nuts at full development.
B. Marketing for Export
5.12     In China, most agricultural foreign trade is conducted by one of
several centralized foreign trade corporations (FTCs) organized along
product lines. These FTCs have their head offices in Beijing, with branch
offices in the main provinces producing or utilizing their products. Also
at the local level are Foreign Trade Bureaus (FTBs) which are in effect
provincial offices of the Ministry of Foreign Economic Relations and Trade
(MOFERT). Two FTCs are involved in the project--The China National
Cereals, Oils and Foodstuffs Import and Export Corporation (CEROILS) and



- 37 -
the China National Native Products and Animal By-products Import and
Export Corporation.  The FTCs conduct all trading activities from the
identification of markets and negotiation of contracts to the procurement
of goods and their shipment. In cooperation with the FTB, buyers are
sought, mostly at the semi-annual Canton (Guangzhou) Trade Fairs.
5.13     Provincial and lower level corporations have little or no part in
negotiating markets and prices. One of the main issues identified in the
Bank report External Trade and Cagital: Reform Issues and Options (Report
No. 6690-CHA) was the "air-lock" that has been created between producers
and the world market. The term "air-lock" describes the buffer that has
been created between Chinese enterprises and world market forces. The
FTCs are the point of contact between China and the world market, and
Chinese enterprises are insulated from world price developments and have
very limited contact with foreign buyers and suppliers. The air-lock
creates four problems which are becoming increasingly important as China
seeks to expand exports: inefficiency of exporting, inappropriate export
patterns, lack of information and lack of competition from imports. In
the case of the present project, lack of information is the most prominent
obstacle. The air-lock of the FTC between the producer and the market has
two serious consequences for the flow of information. First, there is the
simple problem of reacting to market opportunities and secondly, the
absence of contacts between foreign buyers and sellers and Chinese
enterprises means these enterprises are foregoing much "free" technical
assistance concerning such areas as product specifications and design, new
product development, capital equipment, factory design, and quality
control. Foreign experience has shown such direct contacts between
foreign buyers and suppliers and all kinds of domestic enterprises to be
one of the most important, most effective and cheapest sources of
technology transfer and marketing know-how.
5.14     In line with China's policy of gradually giving exporting firms
more autonomy in decision making, each Development Corporation at the
provincial or municipal level will have the option of engaging the FTC as
an agent on their behalf for an agreed commission based on a percentage of
sales value for all export items produced under the project. An
understanding to this effect was obtained at negotiations.
C. Narkets
5.15     Project products are characterized by proven export markets and
high domestic income elasticities of demand. Most project output would be
aimed at the export market, which Jiangsu and Zhejiang already supply with
shrimp, eel and laver.
5.16     Sbrimp:  World production of shrimp in 1985 was around 2.0
million t, with ten major producers accounting for about half of
production. India and China led the list of major producers with each
producing about 10% of the total. Other major producers were Thailand,
US, Indonesia, Norway, Taiwan province, Mexico, Malaysia and Brazil.
World shrimp trade is some 25-30% of total production. Historically,
China's marine catches have been Acetes japonicus, which is used for



- 38 -
shrimp paste or powder and does not enter the market channels of Penaeid
shrimp. Only a minor portion of Chinese production now enters world trade
as headless shell-on product. The project is expected to produce 6,300 t
of processed shrimp for export.
5.17     To date, Japan is the major market for China's export shrimp.
Japan's total imports increased from 160,000 t in 1981 to 212,000 t in
1986, of which China's share increased from less than one percent to about
nine percent (18,700 t) and is expected by the Japanese Ministry of
Agriculture, Forestry and Fishery to reach 14-15 percent in the early
1990s. At the same time, the Ministry predicted that shrimp demand will
increase by about 10,000 - 11,000 t per year through 1990. Only about 20
percent of Japan's total shrimp supply comes from domestic production; the
remainder is imported. As Japan has stabilized its domestic production at
about 50,000 t, any increase in demand is expected to be met by imports.
Japanese buyers have pursued a policy of developing marine shrimp sources
in other countries. However, as marine catches have levelled off, they
are now concentrating their purchases from shrimp farming. Consumers
prefer two types of shrimp for which they pay higher prices, namely
Kuruma, which is P. JaRoniM& (produced in Hong Kong, Japan, Republic of
Korea, East Malaysia, Pakistan, Papua New Guinea, Taiwan Province and
Vietnam) and Taisho, which is P. orientaliJ (produced primarily in
Republic of Korea and China). Demand has been affected by the rising
income levels and by the greater percentage of reals that are consumed at
restaurants. Annual exports from project production would be less than 3%
of Japan's imports.
5.18     Annual imports of shrimp into the US, which hovered around
100,000 t from 1979 to 1983, subsequently began increasing rapidly and
reached a level of 180,000 t in 1986. Per capita consumption is still
just under 2 kg/annum--less than half of that in Japan.  The Chinese share
of the market, which had been negligible during the earlier period, jumped
from 3,500 t in 1985 to 10,700 t (about five percent of the market) in
1986. Chinese shrimp compete with Mexican and Ecuadorian shrimp (about
34% of the market) and imports from Thailand and Taiwan province (about
15% of the market). Thus far, China has not tapped the European markets,
which are supplied to a considerable extent by northern European
countries. Also, Europe imports peeled and cooked & peeled shrimp,
whereas China exports mainly headless, shell-on product.
5.19     Virtually all of China's shrimp is marketed through bulk shipment
contracts entered into at the semi-annual (April and October) Guangzhou
Trade Fairs. Under the project, working groups would be formed to study
and recommend ways in which the marketability of project output could be
increased. They would consider such topics as quality improvement (from
handling at pond side through processing), product diversification
(examples of which would be peeled, cooked & peeled, deveined, head-on,
bread and batter), packaging, and market development (through more active
participation in the world market). For this purpose, it may be
considered desirable for the working groups to develop contacts with
buyers. Funds for overseas study tours would be included in the project.
An assurance was obtained at negotiations that the working groups would be
established with terms of reference acceptable to the Bank Group.



- 39-
5.20     Domestically, the market for project shrimp is expanding.  The
project would have an incremental production of about 4,500 t of
unprocessed shrimp for the local market. Annual per capita consumption of
all fishery products is still in the range of 5 kg, though the increase in
aquaculture activities is expected to raise this level for urban consumers
in the near future. China's per capita incomes are expected to rise in
real terms during the 1987-92 period by over 6% annually, which combined
with favorable elasticities of demand, promises a secure domestic market
for any non-exported products. Shrimp supplies for the domestic market at
the current time are limited to small quantities of freshwater shrimp and
undersized or inferior quality P. orientals.
5.21     Eel: With increased eel production domestically, China is
expected to restrict the export of elvers and concentrate on production of
grown, live or processed eel. Japan is virtually the only market for live
eel, and consumer preference is for eel of 180-220 gms. Larger eel are
mostly exported to Hong KCong. Japan imports about 25% of its annual
demand (in the range of 70,000-80,000 t); China's share of the imports is
slightly less tlan 5%, with the bulk being met by Taiwan province (94%).
Japan also imports roasted eel (about 12,000 t), but here also Taiwan
supplies about 96% and China about 4%, though China's roast eel exports
have increased from 125 t in 1985 to 430 t in 1986. The Japanese roast
eel market, therefore, appears to off6 good possibilities for China and
particularly, the project. The project's total production of 4,810 t
would be exported--about 3,900 t live and 900 t roasted.
5.22     Laver:  The major producers of laver are Japan, Korea, China and
Taiwan province, of which only China is a net exporter. In 1986, Jiangsu
output had reached 215 t, of which 77% was exported. Existing markets
include Southeast Asia, where nori is a popular item in the traditional
diet, and North America and Europe, where the demand due to the restaurant
trade is growing more than 10% per year. About 70% of the project
production is expected to be exported, with the balance to be sold on the
domestic market. Future prospects for marketing appear good in view of
the growing popularity of Japanese cuisine worldwide, increasing
production and consumption of instant meals, and interest in "health"
foods both in the Orient and in the West.
5.23     Livestock products:  Present production of mink in China is
around 3 million skins per year, with about 25% of the production
exported. Jiangsu provides about 15% of total production. Due to poor
genetic quality of the livestock and unstable feed management, skin
production tends to be less than top quality. Producers sell to the China
National Native Produce and Animal By-products Import and Export
Corporation, which negotiates contracts at the semi-annual Guangzhou Trade
Fair and also supplies furs for the major auctions (Copenhagen, Hong Kong,
and London). Market prospects in the U.S., Japan, Italy and West Germany
are promising, and demand is for both male and female furs of medium to
high quality.



- 40 -
5.24     Fruit and nuts:  Fruit production is an underdeveloped sector of
agriculture in Jiangsu. With orchards accounting for only 2.50 of the
province's cultivated land, per capita fruit consumption remains at about
5 kg per year, less than half the national average. Improved living
standards have already led to increasing demand for fruits and fruit
products. Consequently, most of the output will be aimed primarily at the
domestic market. The provincial demand for hawthorn is approximately
30,000 t, of which only about 3.5% is produced in the province. The
market demand for fruit juice is estimated to be over 1,000 t in
Lianyungang City alone. Grape juice would fill part of the provincial
demand for fruit juices and replace a portion of the imports. Jiangsu
currently produces about 80,000 t of apples with several hundred thousand
tons purchased from other provinces. About 500 t of gingko are produced
annually in the project area, of which 300 t are exported to Hong Kong and
other southeast Asian countries.
D. Prices
5.25     In the past, provincial FTCs have been responsible for
procurement and marketing of export products. They have determined prices
to producers and negotiated with buyers, using excessive margins on some
products to offset losses on others. For the project, each municipality
would have the option of continuing to sell its output to the FTC or
engaging the FTC as an agent on a commission basis (para. 5.14). As
production increases, most municipalities are expected to adopt the latter
practice. The prices negotiated with foreign buyers for many products
have been lower than similar products sold by other exporting countries;
part of this may be due to lower quality and part appears to be an effort
to gain a competitive position. It appears that farmers now face contract
prices set by the FTCs which are less than world market prices,
particularly for eel.
5.26     The financial analysis of the project subcomponents has been
conducted using 1987 average free market prices for horticultural and
livestock products and the provincial governments' 1987 contractual prices
for shrimp, eel, laver and mink. For the economic analysis, farm input
and output values are based upon Bank Group commodity price forecasts and
1987 export prices, adjusted for international and domestic transport
costs and expressed in 1987 constant prices. Conversion factors are used
to derive economic prices for items which do not enter into international
trade. The current financial wage rate ranges between Y 2.8 and Y 5.0 per
workday. Given the unemployment and underemployment in various parts of
the coastal areas, the economic value of unskilled labor has been
estimated at about 50% of the financial wage rate multiplied by the
consumption conversion factor of 1.13. Thus, the shadow wage rates
adopted in the economie analysis ranged from Y 1.6 to Y 2.9.  Current and
projected financial and economic prices and conversion factors are
summarized in Annex 7, which also includes financial and economic analyses
and farm budgets.



- 41 -
E. Farm-Budge&
5.27          Shrimp farms would be operated as county, township or
village enterprises under professional management teams with operation of
individual ponds (2-3 ha in size) contracted oUt to farm households with
each worker responsible for 1.67 ha. Income statements and cash flows
were prepared for 1.67 ha holdings of both new and improved ponds, taking
into account a pro rata share of loan repayment and farm management fees
(Annex 7). Operation of this size pond would require about 250 mandays
per year (nearly full-time employment for one worker, paid at the rate of
about Y 1,200 annually). In addition, about 500 mandays would be required
for annual maintenance after the growing season; this would likely be
performed by the worker, other family members and hired labor. Assuming
that the worker's family undertakes 200 days of the annual maintenance,
the family income would be in the range of Y 2250 per year or Y 560 per
capita (based on a family of four). This is about 16% higher than the
average per capita income of Y 480 in the project areas. During the
12-year period of total repayment of the investment cost, earnings for the
enterprise itself would average Y 130/ha and afterwards, about Y
5,800/ha. The same analysis applied to an improved pond shows that it
would generate the same level of income for operators, but, with a much
lower investment cost per unit area, a higher income for the enterprise--
about Y 7,500/ha after loan repayment. Under the present system of
collective ownership and taxation, about half of this would go to the
local government in the form of taxes and the balance would be kept by the
farm for financing other investments and distribution to management staff
and pond operators. This would be in addition to the tax of 5 percent of
gross value of output already levied on producers.
5.28     Eel farms would be operated as enterprises with skilled workers
engaged for production activities. Each ton of market eel would require
about 100 mandays labor. A farm budget for a typical farm with 3,000 m2
of enclosed ponds and 1.4 ha of open ponds would require 5,100 mandays for
production or full time employment for 17 workers (at 300 days per year).
Workers would earn about Y 1,500 per year; the farm itself would generate
a net income of about Y 57,000 annually during the loan repayment period
and over Y 200,000 after the loan is repaid.
5.29     Laver operations are located at the seaside, typically in
villages which depend on their incomes from coastal fishing. Most of the
labor is seasonal, taking place at harvest (60 percent) from December to
April and usually performed by village women. The remaining 40 percent is
required for installation of rafts and nets prior to and dismantling after
the production season. A household, comprised of two laborers and
responsible for about 0.2 ha of laver fields, would have combined annual
earnings of about Y 630 to supplement family earnings from coastal fishing
and farming. Average incomes are low along the coast--about Y 419 per
capita, and earnings from laver would boost per capita incomes by 35% to Y
575. A village with 14 ha of laver, a nursery and a processing facility
for washing and drying to produce a marketable product would generate,
after paying production costs (includirS labor and processing costs), an
annual net income of about Y 83,000 (Y 74,000 from processing operations
and Y 9,000 from production) after the period of loan repayment.



- 42 -
VI. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Prgduction and Benefits
6.1      At full development, annual incremental production would amount
to abovt 14,900 t of shrimp, 4,800 t of eel, and 410 t of dried laver.
Livest ick improvements would produce annually about 130,000 mink, 1
million goats, 1 million rabbits, 460,000 ducks and 1,200 t of fish.
Agricultire activities would provide incremental production of 43,000 t of
fruits and 18,200 t of nuts. Annual incremental outpat from project
investments would be about Y 550 million (US$148 million). This and the
following analysis excludes the financial and economic production benefits
of the Jiangsu agricultural technology development component, which will
comprise eight subprojects (Annex 2), each of which is supported by a
feasibility study. The financial returns of each of these subprojects,
after projected taxes ranging up to 50% of net income and based on
conservative output and input price assumptions and reasonable production
factor assumptions, are in all cases greater than 9%. The direct economic
rates of return, based on the same assumptions, in all cases would exceed
12%. If indirect benefits to the nation are eventually recouped through
dispersion of technological developments, the economic benefits would be
substantial.
B. Emloyment And Incomes
6.2      Employment opportunities generated by the project would help
reduce underemployment among the rural labor force in and around the
project areas. The project would improve living standards and income
levels for a total of about 115,000 workers and their families. About
9,000 households would earn the major part of family income through
full-time employment in mariculture activities. About 21,000 households
would supplement their agricultural incomes with animal husbandry sideline
activities and about 85,000 households would earn additional income from
fruit and nut production. Financial analyses of the typical household
farm income for aquaculture production (para. 5.27-5.29) shows that,
within the first three years of the project, per capita incomes would
increase from 16 to 35 percent above the present levels.
C. Economic and Financial Analyses
6.3      An economic analysis was undertaken for each production
activity. The total investment cost used in the economic analysis
includes physical contingencies, but excludes price contingencies. It
includes the full cost of technical service centers and training of
management staff, even though the benefits of these components would be
extended in the future to farmers outside the project areas.  The Jiangsu
shrimp component was split into new and improved ponds (3,958 and 4,303
ha, respectively), because of the difference in investment costs
associated with the two subcomponents. The Zhejiang shrimp component was
analyzed by geographic area; even though all the areas are small (ranging
in size from 122 to 611 ha), project costs include from 15 to 100% of the
associated sea dike construction. Sea dike construction represents the
main difference in construction costs between the two provinces; in
Jiangsu, sea dikes have been constructed over the last several decades and



- 43 -
in Zhejiang, are being undertaken as part of the project. Economic prices
for shrimp, eel and laver were derived from fob Shanghai prices for
Japanese imports. The difference in economic farmgate prices for shrimp
(Y 12,985/t'in Jiangsu and Y 15,300/t in Zhejiang) reflects the two-week
longer growing season and consequent larger size in the more southern
province. All economic values were converted to local currency at the
official exchange rate effective at negotiations of Y 3.7 - US$1.00. The
economic wage rate for operating mariculture components is estimated at
Y 3.2 per day, equivalent to the expected wage of about Y5 per day,
multiplied by the conversion factors for unskilled labor (0.50) and
consumption (1.13). Using these assumptions and discounting costs and
benefits over 20 years, the economic rates of return (ERRs) for the
mariculture components ranging from 30 to 44% in Jiangsu and from 20 to
52% in Zhejiang. At a discount rate of 12%, which is considered to
approximate the opportunity crbt of capital in China, the net present
value of the mariculture components is Y 521 billion (US$140 billWon).
The EkRs were tested for sensitivity to variations in the cost of
investment (+25%), labor (+25%), and operation (+25%) and price of output
(-10%). The sensitivity analysis shows that the project's economic
viability would remain intact with likely levels of change in any single
variable. ERRs are shown in Table 6.1.
6.4      The livestock and agricultural components were analyzed by
production activity, and the ERRs were tested for sensitivity to
variations as above. ERRs for the base case vary between 16 and 25% for
the agriculture components and between 28 and 43% for animal husbandry.
Sensitivity analyses indicates that these components' economic viability
would remain intact with likely levels of change in any single variable.
6.5      Financial analysis was carried out for each component to insure
that farmers would have adequate incentives to participate in the project
and that agroprocessing enterprises were financially viable. The FRRs for
project components ranged from 12 to 24%, and for agroprocessing from 12
to 40%. Selected financial and economic models are shown in Annex 7;
further details are contained in the Project File.
D. Risks
6.6      The project faces no significant technical or organizational
risks. Should significant improvements occur in the feed conversion ratio
for shrimp, the project's financial and economic viability would be even
further improved. Products to be exported have experienced strong and
rapidly growing demand in Japan and other Southeast Asian markets, as well
as in North America and western Europe. Incremental output from the
project will be relatively small compared to international demand and
trade of these items. The most important determinant of market acceptance
of these items is product quality, and the project design accordingly
includes modern processing facilities for all export items and the
formation of study groups focusing on improving the marketability of
shrimp. Domestic markets are also strong and growing rapidly with
increases in incomes, especially in the coastal provinces and cities.

VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1     At negotiations, assurances were obtained that:
(a) adequate quantities of chemical fertilizer nutrients would be
procured and made available to farmers and that an annual program
for such provision would be prepared and submitted to the Bank
Group for review prior to November 1 of each year (para. 3.22);
(b) the training components would be carried out in accordance with a
schedule acceptable to the Bank Group (paras. 3.31 and 3.39);
(c) provincial project offices would submit the consolidated accounts
for auditing annually by independent auditors acceptable to the
Bank Group and that the audited accounts, with a statement on the
use of SOEs, would be submitted to the Bank Group within six
months of the close of each financial year (para. 3.51);
(d) provincial governments would insure that project activities would
conform to appropriate guidelines on environmental control issued
by the provincial governments and acceptable to the Bank Group
(para. 3.52);
(e)  agroprocessing facilities would be managed by competent staff
with experience and backgrounds relevant to the particular type
of processing (para 4.10);
(f) project funds would be onlent to end users at the same interest
rates charged by ABC on subloans for similar purposes and with
similar maturities and that the interest rate would be subject to
periodic review with the Bank Group (para. 4.11);
(g) the "float",i.e. any net cash accruals by the Development
Corporations due to differences in timing between subloan
receipts and repayments to Government, would be used by them only
for financing development activities similar to those undertaken
under the project (para. 4.12); and
(h) a working group, with terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank
Group, would be established in each province to study
improvements in marketability of shrimp (para. 5.19).
7.2     At negotiations, understandings were reached on the following:
(a)  the operational guidelines for Development Corporation financial
viability, including requirements that a debt-equity ratio of not
greater than 5:1 be maintained, that any additional activities
selected for financing under the project would have projected
financial and economic rates of return of at least 120, and that
sublending to enterprises and farms would be at the prevailing
ABC rates (para. 4.8);



- 46-
(b) the Development Corporations would retain sufficient spreads to
cover their transaction costs, including a reasonable provislon
for bad debts (para. 4.11); and
(c) the Development Corporations at the provincial or municipal level
would have the right to engage the FTC as a sales agent on their
behalf for an agreed commission based on a percentage of sales
value for all export items produced under the project (para.
5.14);
7.3      The conditions for effectiveness w,uld be approval by the State
Council of the Development Credit Agreement and the Loan Agreement and the
execution of the Project Agreement with the Jiangsu and Zhejiang
Provincial Governments.
7.4      Subject to the above conditions, the Coastal Lands Development
Project would constitute a suitable basis for a Bank Loan of $40.0 million
equivalent, with a maturity of 20 years including 5 years of grace, and an
IDA Credit of SDR 44 million ($60.0 million equivalent) on standard IDA
terms, both to the People's Republic of China.

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject I. ShriLn Feed Production Based on Utilizing Marine Fish and
Shrimp Waste
Scale of production:      4,000 t per yr shrimp feed
Total Cost:               Y 2.97 m (US$804,000)
Bank Group Funding:       US$400,000
Commercial Sponsor:       Yancheng Fish Feed Factory, Sheyang County
Technical Sponsor:        Jiangsu Freshwater Fishery Research Institute
Proiect Description:
The proposed feedmill, to be built on the site of a small existing
unit, would introduce both a new shrimp feed formula and a new production
technology to the project area. It would convert 1,200 t per year of
marine fish and shrimp waste (e.g. heads, tails, internal organs) into
liquified protein using an enzymatic digestive process developed by the
Jiangsu Freshwater Fishery Research Institute. Granular feed formulation
would also require 3,600 t of wheat, maize, and grain products all
certified available by the county government. The product reportedly
achieves a 42% protein content (at minimal cost), retains fixed shape in
water for four to six hours (important in efficient feeding and reducing
pond pollution), and permits a 3.1:1 feed oonversion ratio as opposed to
the approximate 5:1 ratio now experienced with conventional local feeds.
Prolect Rationale:
The product would be extremely cheap at the proposed sale price of
Yl,200/t (versus Yl,300-Yl,500/t for conventional feeds) because it
substitutes local fish and shrimp waste for imported animal protein,
mainly fishmeal. The plant is only 5 km from the source of the marine
waste (a seafood processing plant) and 8 km from a major pond complex, so
transport costs are minimal. Removing the waste, which is now dumped near
the source plant, would be environmentally beneficial. Regarding shrimp
production effects, controlled tests on 100 ha of ponds showed an increase
in yields of 17 percent (from 2.1 to 2.48 t/ha), with an increase in
average shrimp size from 11.0 cm to 12.24 cm, with 63 percent of the
output above the local 12 cm export cutoff.

ANNEX 2

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject II. Bei Da II Shrimp Feed Production
Scale of production:      10,000 t per yr shrimp feed
Total Cost:               Y 3.75 m (US$1,013,000)
Bank Group Funding:       US$500,000
Commercial Sponsor:       Haimeng County Feed Factory, Nantong City
Technical Sponsor:        Beijing University Chemistry Department
Project Description:
The project would involve the upgrading of a large animal feed
plant and the introduction of shrimp feed production based on a
proprietary formula recently developed by the Chemistry Department of
Beijing University (Bei Da) under a grant from the China National Natural
Science Fund. The remodeled plant would produce 10,000 t of shrimp feed
during the main shrimp growth season (May - October) and about 10,000 t of
poultry and pig feed during the rest of the year. Beijing University,
which is selling the formula and production technology to the firm for
Y200,000, would also provide the proprietary essences and binders during
production.
Project Rationale:
The product is not as obviously economically attractive as that
proposed by the Yancheng Fish Feed Factory; its costs are substantially
higher as it is conventionally based on imported fish meal and binder for
its animal protein content; the product's protein content is lower (35%),
indeed only marginally acceptable; it guarantees a fixed shape in water
for only two hours; and its tested feed conversion ratio is slightly
higher (3.25:1) although still a marked improvement over conventional
feeds. However, perhaps due to growth stimulants incorporated in the
formula, tests of the product in Shandong province were highly successful,
with an 18 percent increase in shrimp growth, a doubling of export grade
volume, a 22 percent reduction in cost of shrimp production, and low water
contamination (resulting in lower mortality of young shrimp in hot
weather).

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject III. Wink Feed Produ¢tion
Scale of production:         5,000 t per yr mink feed
Total Cost:                  Y 1.99 m (US$539,000)
Bank Group Funding:          US$200,000
Commercial Sponsor:          Chendong Town, Ganyu County, Lianyungang
Municipality
Technical Sponsor:           Nanjing Institute of Grain Economy
Proiect DescriRtion:
The project would involve construction of a new mink feed mill
which would formulate a feed predominantly based on vegetable protein,
developed by the Nanjing Institute of Grain Economy. The formula
substitutes accessible extruded plant protein (derived from soybean,
peanut and corn gluten meal) for a substantial amount of the raw animal
protein (fish and meat) conventionally fed to mink, although one-third of
the feed protein content would still be derived from animal sources (fish
meal, meat scrap, blood meal). Seven different feed formulae will be
produced for various mink life cycle phases.
Project Rationale:
The boom in mink breeding in this coastal county is rapidly
exhausting available supplies of trash fish and crustaceans, which are
also endangered due to overfishing. Mink farmers increasingly have had to
purchase food-grade seafood, which increases their cost of production
markedly, or feed minks raw grain, which reduces pelt quality.
Availability of cheap and effective feed is now the constraint on
profitability and planned expansion of mink farming in the region.
Intensive tests of the product commenced with 640 kits from which 8 males
and 32 females were selected to breed a second generation. A slight
decrease in pelt size was observed, but no reduction in pelt quality
occurred, mortality was reduced, production costs diminished by 35
percent, labor productivity increased several times (due to elimination of
effort in obtaining raw feed, minerals, drugs, etc. separately), and net
revenue per pelt increased by 75 percent. The feed is now being satis-
factorily used on 20,000 minks, 20 percent of the total county stock in
1986.

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELPNERT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject IV: Raneseed Processing Plant
Scale of production:         10,800 t decolloid edible oil and 16,000 t
detoxified rapeseed meal (50% protein
content) per yr /1
Total Cost:                  Y 9,800,000 (US$2,649,000)
Bank Group Funding:          US$1,500,000
Comercial Sponsor:           Hualyang County Oil Processing Company
Technical Sponsor:           Jiangsu Technology Research and Development
Institute/University of Toronto, Department
of Chemical Engineering and Applied Chemistry
Project Descrigtion:
Rebuilding of existing oilseed mill to embody state of the art
Canadian patented technology in association with Jiangsu Technology
Research and Development Institute to detoxify rapeseed meal and improve
quality (through colloid removal) and extraction rate (from 32% to 36-38%)
of edible rapeseed oil. Throughput of the mill would be tripled and
output in value terms would be increased initially six-fold and eventually
nine fold. The process would involve introduction to China of
methano-avmonia and water-hexane two-way extraction and supermembrane
filtration technology to remove glucosinolate toxins, in its first phase.
In the second phase, new techniques to produce isolated and soluble
proteins from rapeseed meal would be introduced. Glucosinolate content
would be reduced from 18 mg/g in raw Chinese rapeseed to 0.4 mg/g in the
meal product. The project provides for a three to six month study term by
four experts to Toronto.
Project Rationale:
Rape is the main oil crop of Jiangsu province and one of the major
sources of edible oil in China. Classical oil pressing technology gives
low recovery, poor quality oil, and a high proportion of cake with a
toxicity level that makes it unsafe as an animal feed, despitR its high
(45*% protein content. About 85 percent of the rapeseed cake in Jiangsu
must, therefore, be used as organic manure, with major negative impacts on
feed availability, the economics of rapeseed production in general, and
the derived economics of rapeseed oil production. These problems exist
with the crop in varying degrees of severity throughout China. The
removal of glucosinolate toxins is thus the most important technological
problem in the feed industry of Jiangsu, indeed, of China. The economic
benefits of this project, if successful, for Huaiyang County (producer of
35 percent of Jiangsu's rapeseed), 'iangsu province, and the nation, go
far beyond the financial benefits of erecting the plant.
/1 Later to be converted to 11,800 t detoxified rapeseed meal (33%
protein content), 2,240 t of separated protein, and 1,920 t of soluble
protein.

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject V: Sesbania Development and Processing
Scale of production:         300 ha of seed fields and 13,300 ha of new
varieties of sesbania (planted on newly
reclaimed tidelands); 2,000 t per yr gum
(glue) powder
Total Cost:                  Y 7,280,000 (US$1,968,000)
Bank Group Funding:          US$980,000
Commercial Sponsor:          Jiangsu Coastal Area Agricultural Science
Institute (Xinyang Station)
Technical Sponsor:           Botanical Research Institute, China Academy
of Agricultural Sciences/Beijing Mining and
Metallurgy Research Institute
Proiect Description:
Sesbania has long been used as a pioneer crop to reclaim highly
saline, newly enclosed tidelands. It is a highly salt tolerant,
nitrogen-fixing legume with obvious advantages as a soil improver and as a
green manure in crop rotation. Commencing in 1973, its seeds have been
collected in China to produce a gum (glue) powder to replace imported guar
glue in an increasing variety of uses. The project would include
development of a seed production base to multiply improved varieties
recently developed by the commercial sponsor (as part of a national
research project); plantings in five coastal counties; expansion of an
existing gum powder factory based on improved technology; and research to
develop varieties with further improvements in nitrogen fixing capability
and gum characteristics closer to those of guar.
Project Rationale:
Sesbania glue (domestic price of Y5,100/t) is increasingly being
used in China to replace guar glue (Y13,00/t) as the hydraulic fracturing
jelly in petroleum wells, the slurry blasting medium in mining explosives,
a mineral concentration flocculant, and in ceramics, paper, battery
production, and dyeing. The gum production technology to be employed was
developed by a series of petroleum and mining research institutes.
National demand from the petroleum and mining industries alone is
reportedly 5,000 t per year, while domestic production is 3,000 t. The
project is intended to fill that expanding gap while simultaneously
improving product quality and substitution ability. The planting program
is probably entirely justified in terms of accelerating improvement of
newly enclosed tidelands (over natural salt leaching), after which
planting bases would gradually shift to other fallow lands.

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVEWPM!ENT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject VI: Barley and Halt DeveloDment and Processing
Scale of production:         130 ha of seed fields, 4,300 ha of production
fields (to supply 14,000 t beer barley), and
new 10,000 t per yr malt plant
Total Cost:                  Y 4,393,000 (US$1,190,000)
Bank Group Funding:          US$590,000
Commercial Sponsor:          Bianchang Township, Yancheng Municipality
Technical Sponsor:           Jiangsu Coastal Area Agricultural Science
Institute (Barley Research Section)
Project Description:
The project would a) create a production base to increase output of
a field-proven, relatively superior beer barley developed by the technical
sponsor; b) construct a malt plant with modified technology to produce
basic brewery material of high standard; and c) fund a research program by
the technical sponsor to further improve malt quality (particularly
germination and milling rates) through breeding of improved barley
varieties.
Project Rationale:
The project, developed under the SPARK program, appears highly
financially profitable and the rationale from the township point of view
is obviously to obtain value added from its established traditional barley
industry (barley is a cotton intercrop) and employ 130 redundant farm
laborers. National beer consumption (4 kg/capita) has increased ten-fold
since 1978, is still increasing at over 10 percent per year, and is widely
seen as a symbol of expanding satisfaction of consumer preferences,
although it is still only one-sixth of the world average. Due to domestic
supply and quality deficiencies, however, one-third (500,000 t) of the
beer barley requirement is now imported. The project would, thus, also
serve an import substitution function. It is also probably well-located,
in a coastal region which already serves as the main domestic source of
beer barley; in a municipality with 20 existing malt plants; and with a
technical sponsor with 30 years experience in barley (including malt
varieties) breeding and agronomy. The combination of factors may
eventually produce the capability to supply a world-class malt to domestic
breweries.

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVEWPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject VII: Dvelogment of Serology Test Kits for Plant and Animal
DiseAsesi
Scale of production:         16,000 animal test kits and 3,500 plant kits
per yr
Total Cost:                  Y 1,860,000 (US$501,000)
Bank Group Funding:          US$250,000
Commercial Sponsor:          Nanjing Agricultural University
Technical Sponsor:           Nanjing Agricultural University
Pro1ect Descriptio:
The project aims to develop serology test kits for accurate and
fast identification of animal and plant diseases particularly viruses.
This would be achieved through the introduction of new techniques and
modern equipment and would result in production of test kits which are
specific for Chinese pathogenic strains and cheaper than the imported
ones. In addition, the test kits would help in early identification of
causal agents of plant and animal diseases prior to their assuming
epidemic proportions. The process depends on injecting healthy animals
with purified proteins of pathological organisms to be tested.
Subsequently, the animals would develop specific antibodies to the
pathogen under consideration and following specific incubation periods for
each pathogen, the animal's blood serum would be processed and purified.
The purified serum would be mixed at known concentrations with proteins of
the specific animal or plant pathogen under question. If the mixture
coagulates, this would indicate the presence of the specific plant or
animal pathoL,n use" frl dvio-14j.-g tha serur. Rentt'ly, C1.ha --d th-
Federal Republic of Germany have established technical cooperation which
includes research, development and training programs between the two
countries. Provision of equipment, reagents and training will be the main
subproject components. Test kits would be mainly used by extension agents
and other research organizations following special training programs.
Project Rationale:
Early, fast and accurate diagnosis of animal and plant disease is
becoming increasingly important in improving animal and plant
productivity. Therefore, the use of serology testing and its
commercialization is important to modernizing China's agriculture and
animal development programs. Nanjing Agricultural University has
successfully developed test kits for diagnosis of swine fever, duck virus
hepatitis, dairy tuberculosis and Newcastle disease. Because of the
speciality of these procedures and the need to refine production methods,
the University will be involved in the pilot production phase of the test
kits which may in the future be produced commercially by specialized
firms.

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Agriculture Technology Development Component
Subproject VIII:  Improvement of AsRaragus Production and Processing
Scale of production:         Planting of 1,000 ha of improved asparagus
and processing of 4,000 t per year
Total Cost:                  Y 4,632,000 (US$1,248,000)
Bank Group Funding:          US$600,000
Commercial Sponsor:          Ganyu County Collective Store and
Agricultural Bureau of Lianyungang City
Technical Sponsor:           Jiangsu Agricultural Science Academy (JASA)
Project Descrintion:
1) Varietal improvement: Asparagus is a dioecious plant, i.e.
separate male and female plants are produced with a ratio of 1:1. Yields
of male plants are usrully higher than female ones (about 20-30%),
depending on the variety, and spears of male plants are more succulent.
To develop improved male varieties, JASA is proposing to use modern
agricultural biotechnology procedures in anther (pollen grain) culture to
produce haploid plants (one set of chromosomes). Treatment of haploid
plants (X or Y) with specific chemicals (e.g. colchicine) will result in
production of diploid fertile supermale plants (YY) and female ones (XX)
which could be multiplied to produce improved breeding lines. Upon
crossing of the homozygous male and female breeding lines, heterozygous
male hybrids will be developed and screened for high yields, disease
resistance and processing quality.
2) Processing development: Modern technology of quick freezing and
canriiug of &aparagus will be introduced. In addition, new technology to
utilize waste during processing and plant tops to produce other
byproducts, e.g. juice, royal jelly and powder, will be experimented with
to assess their commercial feasibility. Total designed capacity of the
proposed processing facilities would be 4,000 t per year for frozen, fresh
and canned asparagus.
Project Rationale:
To increase foreign exchange earnings, production and processing in
Jiangsu Province should be improved by introducing new high-yielding
varieties and modern processing facilities. JASA has recently launched an
asparagus improvement program using modern biotechnology techniques to
produce hybrid varieties. To capitalize on increased yields, this has to
be coupled with efficient processing technology in fast freezing, canning
and other methods. The proposed asparagus breeding and processing
programs to refine production methods are feasible and in line with
China's goals to modernize agricultural production methods. However,
additional research is needed to prove the commercialization of some of
the products envisaged under the proposed subproject.
