CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Productgion Technigues - Mariculture and Mink
Xar.iodture
1.       Shrimn:   Shrimp farms, ranging in size from 20 to 866 ha, would
be comprised of clusters of 2-3.3 ha ponds. The farm would contract with
households for pond operation--generally about 1.67 ha to a worker.
Shrimp production would be based on Ienaeus orientalis, the techniques of
which would be similar in both provinces. The project would provide nine
hatcheries in Jiangsu and three in Zhejiang. Hatcheries would be operated
for a three-month period from March to May. Brood shrimp would be
collected from the wild; spawning (about 40,000 seeds/female) would take
place in large indoor tanks. The fertilized eggs hatch into nauplii
larvae, go through the stages of zoea and mysis before metamorphosing into
postlarvae. Microencapsulated larval shrimp feed is unavailable, and
h,atchery production would depend solely on natural food. Not being
sensitive to light as some of the other penaeids, zoea of this species can
be cultured in tanks together with their food organisms (diatomes).
During the mysis and early postlarval stages, the bulk of the food is
naipili of the brine shrimp (Artemia). About 20 days after hatching the
postlarvae reach the 0.7-1.0 cm size used for stocking.
2.       In mid-May, prior to stocking, ponds would be filled with 60 cm
of water, sterilized with tea seed cake and fertilized with urea (0.3
t/ha), chicken manure (0.15 t/ha), or steer manure (0.3 t/ha). Around the
end of May, ponds would be stocked with 0.7-1.0 cm shrimp seeds at the
rate of 300,000/ha. In July and August, as the shrimp biomass increases
and as waste accumulates in the system, from 10-158 of the pond water
would be replaced daily. In late October, ponds would be drained and
shrimp collected by attaching a net bag to the drain pipe.  From the
initial stocking of 300,000 seeds, about 97,500 individuals would be
harvested--a survival rate of about 33%. The main differences between the
growing conditions in the two provinces are that (1) Zhejiang is expected
to have less of a problem with growth cessation during the hottest summer
months since water supplies would be directly from the sea and thus water
temperature would be lower and (2) Zhejiang, lying further to the south,
has a two week longer growing season than Jiangsu.
3.       Currently most of the feed applied to shrimp ponds is in the form
of fresh, frozen or dried trash fish and molluscs, with a very high
conversion factor and accumulation of waste in the system. Some
additional feed is produced by small on-farm pelletizers from ground,
dried trash fish, soybean cake and grains, with the composition depending
upon availability of raw material at the time. The quality of feed is
inconsistent and inferior and waste of raw materials is high. Although
the quantity of trash fish and molluscs in the provinces is abundant, the
supply is not consistent and hence, the feeding regimen is erratic. An
important part of the project would be the introduction of controlled
feeding practices and pelletized feed. As a result of the deeper ponds
and better water control provided under the project, average yields are
expected to increase from, about 0.9 tA.a to 1.95 t/ha by the third year of



- 104 -
Page 2
production. The yield structure in Jiangsu is projected to be ab
ut 30% in the 13-16 cm size range, 50% in the 10-13 cm range and 20% in
the 8-10 cm range; in Zhejiang, it Is projected to be 48% in the 13-16 cm
size range and 52% in the 10-13 cm range--slightly larger on account of
the longer growing season. Total incremental production at full
development would be around 12,200 t in Jiangsu and 2,700 t in Zhejiang.
4.       Farmers would coordinate harvesting schedules with the cold
storage plant, which would arrange transport from pond-side to the cold
store. At the cold store, processing involves sorting, washing, deheading
and freezing. About 70% of the shrimp are expected to meet the size
requirements for export (10.5 cm in Jiangsu and 12 cm in Zhejiang) and
would be processed in headless, shell-on fo.m. The remainder would be
sold unprocessed in the local market. The levels of processing
mechanization, management and technical skills, and quality control are
generally low. Improved plant design and modern equipment to be provided
under the project would help to remedy some of these problems; improving
quality would be an issue for the working group on marketing.
5.       Laver is a leafy edible seaweed, better known by its Japanese
name, nori.  The species to be cultivated under the project is Pofrhra
yAzoensis Ueda. Porphyra has a two-stage life cycle--the conchocelis
stage and the thallus (edible) stage. In late winter or early spring,
mature thalli produce carpospores which attach to the shells of molluscs,
germinate and penetrate through the calcareous matrix and develop into the
filamentous conchocelis. Following this is the development of
conchosporangia which produce conchospores. Early in the fall, when the
temperature drops, conchospores are released from the mollusc shells,
settle onto another substrate and develop intc the leafy thalli.
6.       Cultivation of Porphyra involves an indoor nursery stage for the
rearing of conchocelis and an outdoor production stage for the growth of
edible thalli. In the nursery phase, around the first of March, mature
thalli are selected and placed in concrete tanks lined with hard clam
shells. The thalli release carpospores which germinate and grow in the
hard clam shells. About 1 sq m of nursery tank is needed for every mu of
laver culture; tanks are generally 7-8 sq m in size. With regular water
changes and manual cleaning of algal growth from the shells, the
conchocelis begin to release conchospores in September. When the density
of conchospores in the nursery tanks :eaches an adequate level, pieces of
5 sq m thalli culture nettings are suspended in the nursery tanks for
conchospores to attach. Seeding is completed in hours, when the settled
conchospores on the netting material reaches adequate density. The
process continues until early October.
7.       The seeded nettings are then removed and mounted in series of
10-20 onto a bamboo raft. The rafts, supported by legs, are anchored
through a rope in the lower part of the intertidal mudflat with the series
of nettings oriented perpendicular to the coastline. During low tide the
rafts sit on the stustrate and during high tide they float in the water.
Every ha of culture surface requires 7 ha of gross area. Conchospores on
the nets germinate into thalli. The first harvest can be performed in
early December when the thalli reach a length of about 0.3 m. Harvesting
is carried out manually during low tide. Thereafter, the nets are
harvested every two weeks until late spring. Early harvests yield about



- 105 -
ANNEX 6
Patg 3
1,050 kg/ha, increasing up to 1,950 kg/ha toward the end of the season,
with an average of 1,500 kg/ha for each of ten harvests. Annual yield is
about 15 t/ha in vet weight or 1.5 t/ha in dried form after processing.
Processing facilities for automated washing and drying of laver would be
provided for each 10-15 ha, generally within 1/2 hr distance from the
culture sites.
8.       &I.  The species used for eel culture in China is the Japanese
eel, Anguilla jaRonica, a catadromous species (grows in freshwater but
migrates to the sea for spawning). The larval eel (leptocephali) spend
about six months in the sea before returning to freshwater. Upon
reaching an estuarine environment, they begin to metamorphose from the
transparent and extremely compressed leptocephalus body form into the
eel-like body form. At the intermediate point of metamorphosis, they have
acquired a transparent eel-like body form and are called glass eels. At
this point, they are collected in estuaries by fish farmers. 1ithin about
a week, the glass eel acquires dark pigmentation and is then called an
elver. Technically, it is still impossible to produce elver in hatcheries
and all eel culture makes use of seeds of wild origin. Glass eel of A,
iagonica occur only along the coast of China, Korea, Japan and Taiwan.
Still, Japan and Taiwan depend heavily on glass eel and elver imported
from Korea and China.
9.       The Japanese eel is a warm-water species, growing only when water
temperature is above 20 degrees C. To compensate for having only a five
month growing season, eel production in Jiangsu makes use of enclosed
ponds and warm water from deep wells, supplemented with artificial
heating, to maintain water temperature within the range of 25-28 degrees C
year round. Eel culture is characterized by a combination of enclosed and
outdoor ponds in a series of different sizes, frequent grading, thinning
and transfer from one pond to another, and multiple size-selective
harvests without any definite seasonality. With aeration and continuous
water replacement, very high stocking density can be maintained. Glass
eel are stocked into the system during February, Narch and April when they
occur in abundance in the elver fisheries. Repeated grading by size is
carried out. From June to October, larger eels are transferred into
outdoor ponds for their final grow out. In late October, underaized eel
are transferred back into enclosed ponds for overwintering. Japanese eels
have highly variable growth rates--fast growers reach market size (150-200
g) in less than 12 munths from glass eel stage, while slow growers may
take more than 24 months. Annual yield is expected to be 4.8 kg/m2 of
enclosed pond and 15 t/ha from outdoor ponds. Both provinces would have
in addition to the standard combination of outdoor and enclosed ponds,
some ponds using slow-flowing power plant waste water (10 ha in Jiangsu
and 40 ha in Zhejiang). By using heated waste water from the power plants,
supplemented with steam from boilers, and cold surface water from
reservoirs during the summer, water temperature will be maintained year
round at 25 - 28 degrees C. The daily rate of water replacement would be
1000 in the winter and 150% during the summer. With proper scheduling of
pond usage in the system and with periodic grading and thinning every
three to six.weeks, yield is projected to be 45 t/ha in Jiangsu and 52.5
t/ha in Zhejiang using the heated flowthrough system. Total annual
production would be 1,660 t in Jiangsu and 3,150 t in Zhejiang.



- 106 -
Page 4
10.      Once harvested, the eel would be held in clear running water for
about 48 hours to condition them for transport. After conditioning, 7.5
kg of market eel would be sealed in a plastic bag with oxygen, water and
ice. The bags would be packed two to a box and delivered to the airport
at a designated time for direct shipment to Japan.
ML%k
11.      ThIe main rationale for this component is the availability of
suitable feed (trash fish, goat and rabbit offal) and an existing industry
of fairly hlgh standard. About 75% of the commercial w'ink would be raised
at the four existing breeding farms to be improved under the project. In
addition, about 90 farm households would be organized into groups of ten,
with each group reL.-,nsible for raising 3500 animals.  The mink breeding
year is characterized by the seasonal work to support production: care of
the animals from December to March, mating season in March, parturition
and lactation in April-May, weaning in June, real growth from July to
September, moulting in September-November, and pelting in
November-December. Essentially responsive to day length, the minks'
mating season is in early- to mid-March. The average gestation period is
about 49 days with births occurring in late April to early May   The
average litter size is 5.1 kits/female with 3.2 surviving to pelting age
of eight months. Slaughter is accomplished by injection of chloral
hydrate (40% solution) into the abdominal cavity or inhalation of carbon
monoxide.
12.      Viability of mink production is dependent on suitable strains of
animals, reproductive performance, feeding and disease resistance.
Genetic disposition, mainly, and feed quality and environment, secondly,
determine color and quality of fur growth with intensity of color of the
guard hairs and underfur being essential characteristics of commercial
value of pelts. Size of the dnimals--and thus of the skin--is also an
important commercial characteristic. The condition for size is good
development from birth to grading time, and a good size at weaning is thus
the principal criterion of good skin size. Size is the easiest single
actor to breed since it is about 40% genetically determined.
13.      Successful mink farming is dependent on breeding animals with
good hereditary characteristics, the availability of good quality feed,
adequate professional skill of the mink breeder and necessary equipment
for raising. The greatest risks to mink production are susceptibility to
viral infections, particularly plasmocytosis (Aleutian disease),
pathogenic bacteria, parasites, fungal infections and chemical toxins.
Few hereditary diseases affect mink production, but aggressive behavior by
siblings or cage mates is a frequeni source of injuries and pelt damage.
At full development, annual project production would be about 140,000
animals. In the past, a major shortcoming in production efficiency was
the lack of grading facilities--pelts were sold to the Foreign Trade
Commission without regard to quality. However, recently grading
procedures have been set up to give farmers more incentive to upgrade care
of animals and resulting pelt quality.                   I

CHINA
COASTAL LANDS DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
OPERATING GUIDELINES FOR JIANGSU AND ZHEJIANG DEVELOPMENT CORPORATIONS
1.  ODerational Principles.  The Jiangsu and Zhejiang Provincial
Development Corporations (DCs) shall:
(a) conform in their organization, financial structure and operations
with the pertinent regulations of the State Council, including the
regulations dated August 14, 1985;
(b) maintain consistency in their lending operations with the policies
and regulations of the Government of China concerning terms for provision
of loans by firancial institutions to farms and rural enterprises;
(c) follow, in their operations, principles of financial management
designed to maintain a surplus of operating revenues over operating
expenses sufficient to safeguard the financial viability of the DCs; and
(d) observe the principles of investment decision making consistent
with the most efficient use of social resources.
2. Snecific Guidelines. The following guidelines are accepted as
practical expressions of the above operational principles, and should be
applied to all DC operations:
(a)  Canitalizat1nn.  DCs should maintain a minimum registered capital
as required by the State Council regulations dated August 14, 1985. As the
DCs increase the size of their debt, the amount of their capital shall be
increased so that the ratio of capital to debt is generally not less than
1:5. The capital increase may be derived from (1) retained earnings; (2)
equity investments, including investments in income-generating fixed assets
(measured at book value ex-depreciation), provided by government, other
economic entities, or individuals; or, for the purpose of this guidelines,
(3) loans received from government which do not carry fixed interest
obligations or fixed repayment periods, but are repayable on terms
determined by the actual future financial performance of the DCs and
subject to approval by the DC Boards of Directors. "Debt" refers to any
loan or credit bearing either fixed interest or fixed maturity other than
short-term credit for working capital purposes. Adequacy of capital in
relation to debt shall be reviewed by the respective Boards of Directors
annually, on the basis of financial performance of DCs during the preceding
year. In assessing the financial performance of DCs, the profitability of
projects financed by the DCs will be taken into account.



- 118 -
ANNE 8
Page 2
(b)  DispositLon of Net Earnings.  Distribution of DC net cash accruals
shall be determined by the Board of Directors only after adequate provision
is made for depreciation of assets, lending or foreign exchange risks, and
wozking capital. Net cash accruals under the Coastal Land Development
Project, due to differences in timing of repayment of loans by farmers,
households and enterprises to DCs, and DC repayments to government, may be
used by DCs, after the above provisions are made, primarily to finance
development similar to that undertaken under the project.
(c)   Investment Decisions.   The decision to finance or participate in
the financing of any economic tctivity other than those described in the
Staff Appraisal Report of the Coastal Lands Development Project shsll
depend on a prior assessment of its technical arA financial feasibility,
which will be conducted using techniques and criteria described in the
Project Appraisal Manuals of either the Agricultural Bank of China or China
Investment Bank. In the case of agroprocessing investments other than
those already appraised by the Bank Group and for which financing is to be
provided under the Coastal Lands Developmert Preject, the feasibility study
must be reviewed and approved by the Bank Group; approval criteria include
a satisfactory study of product market prospects and a minimum financial
aiid economic rate of return of 128.

Selected Documents ,and Data in the Proiect File
A. Project Preparation Regorts and Related Documents
1.   Project preparation notes and component technical feasibility and
appraisal reports (in Chinese) prepared by the State Planning
Commission of Jiangsu and Zhejiang provinces.
2.   Executive Project Summary dated April 27, 1987 and Project Brief
dated March 31, 1987
B. Renorts and Studies Related to the Project
1.  Aquaculture Development in Coastal Lands of Jiangsu and Zhejiang
Provinces of China by Lo-Chai Chen (July 17, 1987).
2.  Appraisal Report on Engineering Component by J.E. Cowley (July
1987).
3. Evaluation of Coastal Lands Development Project (Agroprocessing
Components) by J. Clemmensen (August 1987).
4.  Aquaculture Development in Coastal Lands of Jiangsu and Zhejiang
Provinces (Review of marketing channels and export market outlook)
by S. van Eyes (September 15, 1987).
5.   Mink Production by N. Glem-Hansen (August 1987).
6.   Technical Review of Goat, Duck, Mink Production by J. Doolette
(April, 1987).
7. Environmental Impact Assessment by D. Mossersmith (March 1987).
8.   Note on agricultural component by T.C. Tso (September 16, 1987).
